   <div class="mw-indicators">
   </div>
   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    MMR vaccine controversy
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       MMR vaccine controversy
      </b>
      started with the 1998 publication of a fraudulent research paper in the medical journal
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">
        The Lancet
       </a>
      </i>
      that lent support to the later discredited claim that
      <a href="/wiki/Colitis" title="Colitis">
       colitis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Autism_spectrum" title="Autism spectrum">
       autism spectrum
      </a>
      disorders are linked to the
      <a href="/wiki/MMR_vaccine" title="MMR vaccine">
       combined measles, mumps and rubella (MMR) vaccine
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakefield_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wakefield-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The media have been criticized for their nave reporting and for lending undue credibility to the architect of the fraud,
      <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Wakefield" title="Andrew Wakefield">
       Andrew Wakefield
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Investigations by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">
        Sunday Times
       </a>
      </i>
      journalist
      <a href="/wiki/Brian_Deer" title="Brian Deer">
       Brian Deer
      </a>
      reported that Andrew Wakefield, the author of the original research paper, had multiple undeclared
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conflicts_of_interest" title="Conflicts of interest">
       conflicts of interest
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer04a_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer04a-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent-and-test-results_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent-and-test-results-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      had manipulated evidence,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer2009_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer2009-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and had broken other ethical codes. The
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      paper was partially retracted in 2004, and fully retracted in 2010, when
      <i>
       The Lancet's
      </i>
      editor-in-chief
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Horton_(editor)" title="Richard Horton (editor)">
       Richard Horton
      </a>
      described it as "utterly false" and said that the journal had been "deceived."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wakefield was found guilty by the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council" title="General Medical Council">
       General Medical Council
      </a>
      of serious professional misconduct in May 2010 and was struck off the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council#Registering_doctors_to_practise_in_the_UK" title="General Medical Council">
       Medical Register
      </a>
      , meaning he could no longer practice as a doctor in the UK.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011, Deer provided further information on Wakefield's improper research practices to the British medical journal,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/BMJ" title="BMJ">
        BMJ
       </a>
      </i>
      , which in a signed editorial described the original paper as fraudulent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WakefieldarticleBMJ_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WakefieldarticleBMJ-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeerarticleBMJ_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DeerarticleBMJ-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">
       scientific consensus
      </a>
      is that no
      <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">
       evidence
      </a>
      links the MMR vaccine to the development of autism, and that this vaccine's benefits greatly outweigh its risks.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the initial claims in 1998, multiple large
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidemiologic" title="Epidemiologic">
       epidemiological
      </a>
      studies were undertaken. Reviews of the evidence by the
      <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">
       Centers for Disease Control and Prevention
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC-MMR-autism_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC-MMR-autism-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Pediatrics" title="American Academy of Pediatrics">
       American Academy of Pediatrics
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" title="Institute of Medicine">
       Institute of Medicine
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">
       US National Academy of Sciences
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IOM-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the UK
      <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">
       National Health Service
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMRthefacts_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MMRthefacts-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_Library" title="Cochrane Library">
       Cochrane Library
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cochrane-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      all found no link between the MMR vaccine and autism. While the Cochrane review expressed a need for improved design and reporting of safety outcomes in MMR vaccine studies, it concluded that the evidence of the safety and effectiveness of MMR in the prevention of diseases that still carry a heavy burden of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morbidity" title="Morbidity">
       morbidity
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">
       mortality
      </a>
      justifies its global use, and that the lack of confidence in the vaccine has damaged public health.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cochrane-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autism_omnibus_trial" title="Autism omnibus trial">
       special court
      </a>
      convened in the United States to review claims under the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Vaccine_Injury_Compensation_Program" title="National Vaccine Injury Compensation Program">
       National Vaccine Injury Compensation Program
      </a>
      rejected compensation claims from parents of autistic children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cedillo-v-HHS_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cedillo-v-HHS-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The claims in Wakefield's 1998
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      article were widely reported;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldacre-2008_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goldacre-2008-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      vaccination rates in the UK and Ireland dropped sharply,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntyre_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which was followed by significantly increased incidence of measles and mumps, resulting in deaths and severe and permanent injuries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pepys_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pepys-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Physicians, medical journals, and editors
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJLiftsCurtain_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BMJLiftsCurtain-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GodleeEditorial_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GodleeEditorial-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJBlogDeer_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BMJBlogDeer-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBTimes_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IBTimes-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WebMDBroyd_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WebMDBroyd-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      have described Wakefield's actions as fraudulent and tied them to epidemics and deaths,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgeOld_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AgeOld-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jasek_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jasek-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a 2011 journal article described the vaccineautism connection as "the most damaging medical hoax of the last 100 years".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klaherty_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Klaherty-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20">

           <span class="tocnumber">

           <span class="toctext">
            Impact on society
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21">
        <a href="#References">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          7
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          References
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22">
        <a href="#Further_reading">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          8
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          Further reading
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/MMR_vaccine#Safety" title="MMR vaccine">
       MMR vaccine safety
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Concerns_about_the_Urabe_strain">
       Concerns about the Urabe strain
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Concerns about the Urabe strain">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Before the autism-related controversy started in 1998, some concern had already arisen about the safety of the MMR vaccine due to side effects associated with the Urabe
      <a href="/wiki/Mumps" title="Mumps">
       mumps
      </a>
      strain, including rare adverse events of
      <a href="/wiki/Aseptic_meningitis" title="Aseptic meningitis">
       aseptic meningitis
      </a>
      , a transient mild form of viral meningitis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schattner_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schattner-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A late-1980s trial in Britain of a form of the MMR vaccine containing the Urabe mumps strain produced three cases of probably associated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Febrile_convulsions" title="Febrile convulsions">
       febrile convulsions
      </a>
      per 1,000 vaccinations. Concerns about adverse reactions to the vaccine were raised by American and Canadian authorities based on reports from Japan linking Urabe MMR with
      <a href="/wiki/Meningoencephalitis" title="Meningoencephalitis">
       meningoencephalitis
      </a>
      . In early 1988, Canadian authorities suspended distribution of the Urabe-based MMR and eventually recalled the product.
     </p>
     <p>
      The UK
      <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">
       National Health Service
      </a>
      introduced an MMR vaccine using the Urabe mumps strain in 1988, replacing it entirely with the Jeryl Lynn strain in September 1992 following the identification of an unacceptable risk of
      <a href="/wiki/Aseptic_meningitis" title="Aseptic meningitis">
       aseptic meningitis
      </a>
      1535 days after vaccination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7483800_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid7483800-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Decisions taken by British authorities at this time were to be criticized later following documents obtained under the
      <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_2000" title="Freedom of Information Act 2000">
       Freedom of Information Act
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With no such risk seen in vaccines using the
      <a href="/wiki/Jeryl_Lynn" title="Jeryl Lynn">
       Jeryl Lynn
      </a>
      mumps strain,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7483800_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid7483800-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colville_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Colville-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the UK NHS withdrew two of the three MMR vaccines then available (Immravax, made by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merieux_UK" title="Merieux UK">
       Merieux UK
      </a>
      , and Pluserix, made by
      <a href="/wiki/GlaxoSmithKline" title="GlaxoSmithKline">
       SmithKline Beecham
      </a>
      ) in favor of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merck_Sharp_and_Dohme" title="Merck Sharp and Dohme">
       Merck Sharp and Dohme
      </a>
      's MMR II brand, based on the Jeryl Lynn strain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colville_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Colville-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although MMR administration did continue with MMR II, the MMR vaccination rate first began to fall after 1996, following claims by Wakefield that it was linked to the
      <a href="/wiki/Inflammatory_bowel_disease" title="Inflammatory bowel disease">
       inflammatory bowel disease
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">
       Crohn's disease
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Urabe strain remains in use in a number of countries; MMR with the Urabe strain is much cheaper to manufacture than with the
      <a href="/wiki/Jeryl_Lynn" title="Jeryl Lynn">
       Jeryl Lynn
      </a>
      strain,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a strain with higher
      <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">
       efficacy
      </a>
      along with a somewhat higher rate of mild side effects may still have the advantage of reduced incidence of overall adverse events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colville_30-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Colville-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Revaccination_campaign">
       Revaccination campaign
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Revaccination campaign">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In the wake of the measles outbreaks, which occurred in England in 1992, and on the basis of analyses of seroepidemiological data combined with mathematical modeling, British Health authorities predicted a major resurgence of measles in school-age children. Two strategies were then examined: either to target vaccination at all children without a history of prior measles vaccination or to immunize all children irrespective of vaccination history.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In November 1994, the latter option was chosen and a national measles and rubella vaccination campaign, described as "one of the most ambitious vaccination initiatives that Britain has undertaken" was commenced:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      within one month, 92% of the 7.1 million schoolchildren in England aged 516 years received measles and rubella (MR) vaccine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="MMR_litigation_starts">
       MMR litigation starts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: MMR litigation starts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In April 1994, Richard Barr,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a solicitor, succeeded in winning legal aid for the pursuit of a class action against the manufacturers of MMR vaccines under the UK
      <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Protection_Act_1987" title="Consumer Protection Act 1987">
       Consumer Protection Act 1987
      </a>
      . The class action case was aimed at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aventis_Pasteur" title="Aventis Pasteur">
       Aventis Pasteur
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SmithKlineBeecham" title="SmithKlineBeecham">
       SmithKlineBeecham
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Merck_%26_Co." title="Merck &amp; Co.">
       Merck
      </a>
      , manufacturers respectively of Immravax, Pluserix-MMR and MMR II.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This suit, based on a claim that MMR is a defective product and should not have been used, was the first big class action lawsuit funded by the Legal Aid Board (which became the
      <a href="/wiki/Legal_Services_Commission" title="Legal Services Commission">
       Legal Services Commission
      </a>
      , which in turn was replaced by the
      <a href="/wiki/Legal_Aid_Agency" title="Legal Aid Agency">
       Legal Aid Agency
      </a>
      ) after its formation in 1988. Noticing two publications from Andrew Wakefield that explored the role of measles virus in
      <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">
       Crohn's disease
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Inflammatory_bowel_disease" title="Inflammatory bowel disease">
       inflammatory bowel disease
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Barr contacted Wakefield for his expertise. According to Wakefield supporters, the two men first met on 6 January 1996.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Legal Services Commission halted proceedings in September 2003, citing a high probability of failure based on the medical evidence, bringing an end to the first case of research funding by the LSC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitz2004_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitz2004-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1998_Lancet_paper">
       1998
       <i>
        Lancet
       </i>
       paper
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1998 Lancet paper">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In February 1998, a group led by
      <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Wakefield" title="Andrew Wakefield">
       Andrew Wakefield
      </a>
      published a fraudulent paper in the respected British medical journal
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">
        The Lancet
       </a>
      </i>
      , supported by a press conference
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMBOreports_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EMBOreports-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Free_Hospital" title="Royal Free Hospital">
       Royal Free Hospital
      </a>
      in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LancetPR_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LancetPR-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This paper reported on twelve children with developmental disorders referred to the Royal Free Hospital. The parents or physicians of eight of these children were said to have linked the start of behavioral symptoms to MMR vaccination. The paper described a collection of bowel symptoms,
      <a href="/wiki/Endoscopy" title="Endoscopy">
       endoscopy
      </a>
      findings and
      <a href="/wiki/Biopsy" title="Biopsy">
       biopsy
      </a>
      findings that were said to be evidence of a possible novel syndrome that Wakefield would later call
      <a href="/wiki/Autistic_enterocolitis" title="Autistic enterocolitis">
       autistic enterocolitis
      </a>
      , and recommended further study into the possible link between the condition and the MMR vaccine. The paper suggested that the connection between autism and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal" title="Gastrointestinal">
       gastrointestinal
      </a>
      pathologies was real, but said it did not prove an association between the MMR vaccine and autism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakefield_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wakefield-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the press conference before the paper's publication, later criticized as "
      <a href="/wiki/Science_by_press_conference" title="Science by press conference">
       science by press conference
      </a>
      ",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMBOreports_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-EMBOreports-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wakefield said that he thought it prudent to use single vaccines instead of the MMR triple vaccine until this could be ruled out as an environmental trigger; parents of eight of the twelve children studied were said to have blamed the MMR vaccine, saying that symptoms of autism had set in within days of vaccination at approximately 14 months. Wakefield said, "I can't support the continued use of these three vaccines given in combination until this issue has been resolved."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer-so-far_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer-so-far-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a video news release issued by the hospital to broadcasters in advance of the press conference, he called for MMR to be "suspended in favour of the single vaccines."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a BBC interview Wakefield's mentor
      <a href="/wiki/Roy_Pounder" title="Roy Pounder">
       Roy Pounder
      </a>
      , who was not a coauthor, "admitted the study was controversial". He added: "In hindsight it may be a better solution to give the vaccinations separately,... When the vaccinations were given individually there was no problem."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These suggestions were not supported by Wakefield's coauthors nor by any scientific evidence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PM-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The limited initial press coverage of the story was reasonable for a small and not very significant study. The
      <i>
       Guardian
      </i>
      and the
      <i>
       Independent
      </i>
      reported it on their front pages, while the
      <i>
       Daily Mail
      </i>
      only gave the story a minor mention in the middle of the paper, and the
      <i>
       Sun
      </i>
      did not cover it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldacre-2008_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goldacre-2008-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Wakefield_Lancet_paper_controversy">
       Wakefield
       <i>
        Lancet
       </i>
       paper controversy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wakefield Lancet paper controversy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The controversy began to gain momentum in 2001 and 2002, after Wakefield published papers which suggested that the immunisation programme was not safe. These were a review paper with no new evidence, published in a minor journal, and two papers on laboratory work which he claimed showed that measles virus had been found in tissue samples taken from children who had autism and bowel problems. There was wide media coverage including distressing anecdotal evidence from parents, and political coverage attacking the health service and government peaked with unmet demands that Prime minister
      <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">
       Tony Blair
      </a>
      reveal whether his infant son, Leo, had been given the vaccine. It was the biggest science story of 2002, with 1257 articles mostly written by non-expert commentators. In the period January to September 2002, 32% of the stories written about MMR mentioned Leo Blair, as opposed to only 25% which mentioned Wakefield. Less than a third of the stories mentioned the overwhelming evidence that MMR is safe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldacre-2008_15-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goldacre-2008-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The paper, press conference and video sparked a major health scare in the United Kingdom. As a result of the scare, full confidence in MMR fell from 59% to 41% after publication of the Wakefield research. In 2001, 26% of family doctors felt the government had failed to prove there was no link between MMR and autism and bowel disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halvorsen_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Halvorsen-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In his book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bad_Science_(book)" title="Bad Science (book)">
        Bad Science
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-book-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ben_Goldacre" title="Ben Goldacre">
       Ben Goldacre
      </a>
      describes the MMR vaccine scare as one of the "three all-time classic bogus science stories" by the British newspapers (the other two are the Arpad
      <a href="/wiki/Pusztai_affair" title="Pusztai affair">
       Pusztai affair
      </a>
      about genetically modified crops, and Chris Malyszewicz and the
      <a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">
       MRSA
      </a>
      hoax).
     </p>
     <p>
      Confidence in the MMR vaccine increased as it became clearer that Wakefield's claims were unsupported by scientific evidence. A 2003 survey of 366 family doctors in the UK reported that 77% of them would advise giving the MMR vaccine to a child with a close family history of autism, and that 3% of them thought that autism could sometimes be caused by the MMR vaccine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A similar survey in 2004 found that these percentages changed to 82% and at most 2%, respectively, and that confidence in MMR had been increasing over the previous two years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A factor in the controversy is that only the combined vaccine is available through the UK National Health Service. As of 2010 there are no single vaccines for measles, mumps and rubella licensed for use in the UK.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prime minister Tony Blair gave support to the programme, arguing that the vaccine was safe enough for his own son, Leo,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but refusing on privacy grounds to state whether Leo had received the vaccine; in contrast, the subsequent Prime Minister,
      <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">
       Gordon Brown
      </a>
      , explicitly confirmed that his son has been immunised.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Cherie_Blair" title="Cherie Blair">
       Cherie Blair
      </a>
      confirmed that Leo had been given the MMR vaccination when promoting her autobiography.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldacre-2008_15-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goldacre-2008-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Administration of the combined vaccine instead of separate vaccines decreases the risk of children catching the disease while waiting for full immunisation coverage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMR-vs-single_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MMR-vs-single-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The combined vaccine's two injections results in less pain and distress to the child than the six injections required by separate vaccines, and the extra clinic visits required by separate vaccinations increases the likelihood of some being delayed or missed altogether;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMR-vs-single_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MMR-vs-single-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      vaccination uptake significantly increased in the UK when MMR was introduced in 1988.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMR-vs-single_56-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MMR-vs-single-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Health professionals have heavily criticized media coverage of the controversy for triggering a decline in vaccination rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is no scientific basis for preferring separate vaccines, or for using any particular interval between separate vaccines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick04_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick04-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      John Walker-Smith, a coauthor of Wakefield's report and a supporter of the MMR vaccine, wrote in 2002 that epidemiology has shown that MMR is safe in most children, but observed that epidemiology is a blunt tool and studies can miss at-risk groups that have a real link between MMR and autism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, if a rare subtype of autism were reliably identified by clinical or pathological characteristics, epidemiological research could address the question whether MMR causes that autism subtype.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is no scientific evidence that MMR causes damage to the infant immune system, and there is much evidence to the contrary.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick04_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick04-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2001, Berelowitz, one of the co-authors of the paper, said "I am certainly not aware of any convincing evidence for the hypothesis of a link between MMR and autism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Paediatric_Society" title="Canadian Paediatric Society">
       Canadian Paediatric Society
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">
       Centers for Disease Control and Prevention
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC-MMR-autism_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CDC-MMR-autism-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" title="Institute of Medicine">
       Institute of Medicine
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">
       National Academy of Sciences
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IOM-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the UK
      <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">
       National Health Service
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMRthefacts_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MMRthefacts-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      have all concluded that there is no evidence of a link between the MMR vaccine and autism, and a 2011 journal article described the vaccineautism connection as "the most damaging medical hoax of the last 100 years".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klaherty_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Klaherty-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_of_interest">
       Conflict of interest
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Conflict of interest">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In February 2004, after a four-month investigation, reporter
      <a href="/wiki/Brian_Deer" title="Brian Deer">
       Brian Deer
      </a>
      wrote in
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times_(UK)" title="The Sunday Times (UK)">
        The Sunday Times
       </a>
      </i>
      of London that, prior to submitting his paper to
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      , Wakefield had received
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">
       
      </a>
      55,000 from
      <a href="/wiki/Legal_Services_Commission" title="Legal Services Commission">
       Legal Aid Board
      </a>
      solicitors seeking evidence to use against vaccine manufacturers, that several of the parents quoted as saying that MMR had damaged their children were also litigants, and that Wakefield did not inform colleagues or medical authorities of the conflict of interest. When the editors of
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      learned about this, they said that based on Deer's evidence, Wakefield's paper should have never been published because its findings were "entirely flawed."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer04a_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer04a-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although Wakefield maintained that the legal aid funding was for a separate, unpublished study
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (a position later rejected by a panel of the UK
      <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council" title="General Medical Council">
       General Medical Council
      </a>
      ), the editors of
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      judged that the funding source should have been disclosed to them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Horton_(editor)" title="Richard Horton (editor)">
       Richard Horton
      </a>
      , the editor-in-chief, wrote, "It seems obvious now that had we appreciated the full context in which the work reported in the 1998
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      paper by Wakefield and colleagues was done, publication would not have taken place in the way that it did."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several of Dr. Wakefield's co-researchers also strongly criticized the lack of disclosure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer04a_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer04a-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Deer continued his reporting in a
      <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">
       Channel 4
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dispatches_(TV_series)" title="Dispatches (TV series)">
       Dispatches
      </a>
      television documentary,
      <i>
       MMR: What They Didn't Tell You
      </i>
      , broadcast on 18 November 2004. This documentary alleged that Wakefield had applied for patents on a vaccine that was a rival of the MMR vaccine, and that he knew of test results from his own laboratory at the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Free_Hospital" title="Royal Free Hospital">
       Royal Free Hospital
      </a>
      that contradicted his own claims.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent-and-test-results_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent-and-test-results-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wakefield's patent application was also noted in
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Offit" title="Paul Offit">
       Paul Offit
      </a>
      's 2008 book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Autism%27s_False_Prophets" title="Autism's False Prophets">
        Autism's False Prophets
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In January 2005, Wakefield sued Channel 4, 20/20 Productions, and the investigative reporter Brian Deer, who presented the Dispatches programme. However, after two years of litigation, and the revelation of more than 400,000 in undisclosed payments by lawyers to Wakefield, he discontinued his action and paid all the defendants' costs.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2006, Deer reported in
      <i>
       The Sunday Times
      </i>
      that Wakefield had been paid 435,643, plus expenses, by British trial lawyers attempting to prove that the vaccine was dangerous, with the undisclosed payments beginning two years before the
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      paper's publication.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This funding came from the UK legal aid fund, a fund intended to provide legal services to the poor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer-so-far_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer-so-far-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Retraction_of_an_interpretation">
       Retraction of an interpretation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Retraction of an interpretation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      and many other medical journals require papers to include the authors' conclusions about their research, known as the "interpretation". The summary of the 1998
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      paper ended as follows:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       <b>
        Interpretation
       </b>
       We identified associated gastrointestinal disease and developmental regression in a group of previously normal children, which was generally associated in time with possible environmental triggers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakefield_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wakefield-1">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         1
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      In March 2004, immediately following the news of the conflict of interest allegations, ten of Wakefield's 12 coauthors retracted this interpretation,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-retraction_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-retraction-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while insisting that the possibility of a distinctive gastrointestinal condition in children with autism merited further investigation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitz2004_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitz2004-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, a separate study of children with gastrointestinal disturbances found no difference between those with
      <a href="/wiki/Autism_spectrum" title="Autism spectrum">
       autism spectrum
      </a>
      disorders and those without, with respect to the presence of measles virus
      <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">
       RNA
      </a>
      in the bowel; it also found that gastrointestinal symptoms and the onset of autism were unrelated in time to the administration of MMR vaccine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Manipulation_of_data">
       Manipulation of data
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Manipulation of data">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On 8 February 2009, Brian Deer reported in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">
        The Sunday Times
       </a>
      </i>
      that Wakefield had "fixed" results and "manipulated" patient data in his 1998 paper, creating the appearance of a link with autism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deer2009_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deer2009-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wakefield denied these allegations,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and even filed a complaint with the
      <a href="/wiki/Press_Complaints_Commission" title="Press Complaints Commission">
       Press Complaints Commission
      </a>
      (PCC)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      over this article on 13 March 2009. The complaint was expanded by a 20 March 2009 addendum by Wakefield's publicist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In July 2009, the PCC stated that it was staying any investigation regarding the
      <i>
       Times
      </i>
      article, pending the conclusion of the GMC investigation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCC_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PCC-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the event, Wakefield did not pursue his complaint, which Deer published with a statement that he and The Sunday Times rejected it as "false and disingenuous in all material respects", and that the action had been suspended by the PCC in February 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="General_Medical_Council_investigation">
       General Medical Council investigation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: General Medical Council investigation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council" title="General Medical Council">
       General Medical Council
      </a>
      (GMC), which is responsible for licensing doctors and supervising medical ethics in the UK, investigated the affair.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The GMC brought the case itself, not citing any specific complaints, claiming that an investigation was in the public interest. The then-secretary of state for health, John Reid MP, called for a GMC investigation, an investigation Wakefield seems himself to have wished.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During a debate in the House of Commons, on 15 Mar 2004, Dr. Evan Harris,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a Liberal Democrat MP, called for a judicial inquiry into the ethical aspects of the case, even suggesting it might be conducted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_Prosecution_Service" title="Crown Prosecution Service">
       CPS
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2006 the GMC confirmed that they would hold a disciplinary hearing of Wakefield.
     </p>
     <p>
      The GMC's Fitness to Practise Panel first met on 16 July 2007
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to consider the cases of Dr. Wakefield, Professor John Angus Walker-Smith, and Professor Simon Harry Murch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      All faced charges of serious professional misconduct. The GMC examined, among other ethical points, whether Wakefield and his colleagues obtained the required approvals for the tests they performed on the children; the data-manipulation charges reported in the
      <i>
       Times
      </i>
      , which surfaced after the case was prepared, were not at question in the hearings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The GMC stressed that it would not be assessing the validity of competing scientific theories on MMR and autism. The General Medical Council alleged that the trio acted unethically and dishonestly in preparing the research into the MMR vaccine. They denied the allegations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The case proceeded in front of a GMC Fitness to Practise panel of three medical and two lay members.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 28 January 2010, the GMC panel delivered its decision on the facts of the case: Wakefield was found to have acted "dishonestly and irresponsibly" and to have acted with "callous disregard" for the children involved in his study, conducting unnecessary and invasive tests.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The panel found that the trial was improperly conducted without the approval of an
      <a href="/wiki/Institutional_review_board" title="Institutional review board">
       independent ethics committee
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and that Wakefield had multiple undeclared
      <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">
       conflicts of interest
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Full_retraction_and_fraud_allegations">
       Full retraction and fraud allegations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Full retraction and fraud allegations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In response to the GMC investigation and findings, the editors of
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      announced on 2 February 2010 that they "fully retract this paper from the published record."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-full-retraction_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-full-retraction-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Hansard" title="Hansard">
       Hansard
      </a>
      text for 16 March 2010 reported
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ian_McColl,_Baron_McColl_of_Dulwich" title="Ian McColl, Baron McColl of Dulwich">
       Lord McColl
      </a>
      asking the Government whether it had plans to recover legal aid money paid to the experts in connection with the measles, mumps and rubella/measles and rubella vaccine litigation. Lord Bach, Ministry of Justice dismissed this possibility.
     </p>
     <p>
      In an April 2010 report in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/BMJ" title="BMJ">
        BMJ
       </a>
      </i>
      , Deer expanded on the laboratory aspects of his findings recounting how normal clinical
      <a href="/wiki/Histopathology" title="Histopathology">
       histopathology
      </a>
      results generated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Free_Hospital" title="Royal Free Hospital">
       Royal Free Hospital
      </a>
      were later changed in the medical school to abnormal results, published in The Lancet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enterocolitis_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-enterocolitis-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deer wrote an article in the
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      casting doubt on the "autistic enterocolitis" which Wakefield claimed to have discovered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enterocolitis_90-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-enterocolitis-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the same edition, Deirdre Kelly, President of the European Society of Pediatric Gastroenterology and Nutrition and the Editor of the Journal of Pediatric Gastroenterology and Nutrition expressed some concern about the
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      publishing this article while the GMC proceedings were underway.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 24 May 2010, the GMC panel found Wakefield guilty of serious professional misconduct on four counts of dishonesty and 12 involving the abuse of developmentally challenged children, and ordered that he be struck off the medical register.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times25510_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-times25510-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      John Walker-Smith was also found guilty of serious professional misconduct and struck off the medical register, but was exonerated in 2012 after successfully appealing to the High Court, because the GMC panel had failed to decide whether Walker-Smith actually thought he was doing research in the guise of clinical investigation and treatment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Simon Murch was found not guilty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times25510_92-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-times25510-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 5 January 2011, the
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      published the first of a series of articles by Brian Deer, detailing how Wakefield and his colleagues had faked some of the data behind the 1998 Lancet article. By looking at the records and interviewing the parents, Deer found that for all 12 children in the Wakefield study, diagnoses had been tweaked or dates changed to fit the article's conclusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Continuing the
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      series on 11 January 2011,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowMakeMoney_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HowMakeMoney-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deer said that based upon documents he obtained under
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_information_legislation" title="Freedom of information legislation">
       Freedom of information legislation
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WakefieldCapitalize_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WakefieldCapitalize-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wakefieldin partnership with the father of one of the boys in the studyhad planned to launch a venture on the back of an MMR vaccination scare that would profit from new medical tests and "litigation driven testing".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AuthorHeld_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AuthorHeld-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       The Washington Post
      </i>
      reported that Deer said that Wakefield predicted he "could make more than $43 million a year from diagnostic kits" for the new condition,
      <a href="/wiki/Autistic_enterocolitis" title="Autistic enterocolitis">
       autistic enterocolitis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WakefieldCapitalize_96-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WakefieldCapitalize-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/WebMD" title="WebMD">
       WebMD
      </a>
      reported on Deer's
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      report, saying that the $43 million predicted yearly profits would come from marketing kits for "diagnosing patients with autism" and that "the initial market for the diagnostic will be litigation-driven testing of patients with AE [autistic enterocolitis, an unproven condition concocted by Wakefield] from both the UK and the USA".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoctorPlanned_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DoctorPlanned-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to WebMD, the
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      article also claimed that the venture would succeed in marketing products and developing a replacement vaccine if "public confidence in the MMR vaccine was damaged".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoctorPlanned_98-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DoctorPlanned-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Media_role">
       Media role
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Media role">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Observers have criticized the involvement of mass media in the controversy, what is known as '
      <a href="/wiki/Science_by_press_conference" title="Science by press conference">
       science by press conference
      </a>
      ',
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMBOreports_42-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-EMBOreports-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      alleging that the media provided Wakefield's study with more credibility than it deserved. A March 2007 paper in
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BMC_Public_Health" title="BMC Public Health">
        BMC Public Health
       </a>
      </i>
      by Shona Hilton, Mark Petticrew, and Kate Hunt postulated that media reports on Wakefield's study had "created the misleading impression that the evidence for the link with autism was as substantial as the evidence against".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hilton_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hilton-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Earlier papers in
      <i>
       Communication in Medicine
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" title="British Medical Journal">
        British Medical Journal
       </a>
      </i>
      concluded that media reports provided a misleading picture of the level of support for Wakefield's hypothesis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Speers_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Speers-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacson_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jacson-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dobson_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dobson-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A 2007 editorial in
      <i>
       Australian Doctor
      </i>
      complained that some journalists had continued to defend Wakefield's study even after
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      had published the retraction by 10 of the study's 12 original authors, but noted that it was an investigative journalist, Brian Deer, who had played a leading role in exposing weaknesses in the study.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katelaris_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Katelaris-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/PRWeek" title="PRWeek">
        PRWeek
       </a>
      </i>
      noted that after Wakefield was removed from the general medical register for misconduct in May 2010, 62% of respondents to a poll regarding the MMR controversy stated they did not feel that the media conducted responsible reporting on health issues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRWeek_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PRWeek-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="New England Journal of Medicine">
        New England Journal of Medicine
       </a>
      </i>
      article examining the history of antivaccinationists said that opposition to vaccines has existed since the 19th century, but "now the antivaccinationists' media of choice are typically television and the Internet, including its social media outlets, which are used to sway public opinion and distract attention from scientific evidence".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgeOld_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AgeOld-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The editorial characterized anti-vaccinationists as people who "tend toward complete mistrust of government and manufacturers, conspiratorial thinking, denialism, low cognitive complexity in thinking patterns, reasoning flaws, and a habit of substituting emotional anecdotes for data", including people who range from those "unable to understand and incorporate concepts of risk and probability into science-grounded decision making" and those "who use deliberate mistruths, intimidation, falsified data, and threats of violence".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgeOld_23-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-AgeOld-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a January 2011 editorial in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_American_Spectator" title="The American Spectator">
        The American Spectator
       </a>
      </i>
      , Robert M. Goldberg contended that evidence from the scientific community of issues with Wakefield's research " ... were undermined because the media allowed Wakefield and his followers to discredit the findings just by saying so".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Fiona_Godlee" title="Fiona Godlee">
       Fiona Godlee
      </a>
      , editor of the
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      said in January 2011:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "The original paper has received so much media attention, with such potential to damage public health, that it is hard to find a parallel in the history of medical science. Many other medical frauds have been exposed but usually more quickly after publication and on less important health issues."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GodleeEditorial_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-GodleeEditorial-19">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         19
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Concerns have also been raised over the journal peer-review system, which largely relies on trust among researchers,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJLiftsCurtain_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BMJLiftsCurtain-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the role of journalists reporting on scientific theories that they "are hardly in a position to question and comprehend".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBTimes_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IBTimes-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Neil Cameron, a historian who specializes in the history of science, writing for The
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Gazette_(Montreal)" title="The Gazette (Montreal)">
        Montreal Gazette
       </a>
      </i>
      labeled the controversy a "failure of journalism" that resulted in unnecessary deaths, saying that 1)
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      should not have published a study based on "statistically meaningless results" from only 12 cases; 2) the anti-vaccination crusade was continued by the satirical
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_Eye_(magazine)" title="Private Eye (magazine)">
        Private Eye
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine; and 3) a grapevine of worried parents and "nincompoop" celebrities fueled the widespread fears.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FailureJournalism_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FailureJournalism-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       Gazette
      </i>
      also reported that:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       "There is no guarantee that debunking the original study is going to sway all parents. Medical experts are going to have to work hard to try to undo the damage inflicted by what is apparently a rogue medical researcher whose work was inadequately vetted by a top-ranked international journal."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FalseStudy_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FalseStudy-107">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         107
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Litigation">
       Litigation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Litigation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Litigations are parts of the controversy in different ways. During the 1980s and 1990s, a number of lawsuits were brought in the United States against manufacturers of vaccines, alleging the vaccines had caused physical and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">
       mental disorders
      </a>
      in children. While these lawsuits were unsuccessful, they did lead to a large jump in the costs of the MMR vaccine, and pharmaceutical companies sought legislative protections. In 1993,
      <a href="/wiki/Merck_KGaA" title="Merck KGaA">
       Merck KGaA
      </a>
      became the only company willing to sell MMR vaccines in the United States and the United Kingdom.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">
       Italy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Italy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In June 2012, a local court in
      <a href="/wiki/Rimini" title="Rimini">
       Rimini
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
