       Italy
      </a>
      , ruled that the MMR vaccination had caused autism in a 15-month-old boy. The court relied heavily on the discredited
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      paper and largely ignored the scientific evidence presented to it. The decision was appealed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On February 13, 2015, the decision was overturned by a Court of Appeals in Bologna.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bocci_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bocci-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">
       Japan
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Japan">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Urabe strain, which was different from the strain used in UK,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whyjapan_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-whyjapan-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      was introduced in 1989 and discontinued in 1993 due to causing a high incidence of
      <a href="/wiki/Aseptic_meningitis" title="Aseptic meningitis">
       aseptic meningitis
      </a>
      and other low-level disorders. It was replaced by the three different vaccines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whyjapan_110-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-whyjapan-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naoko_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-naoko-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The problems were caused by the anti-mumps component.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andy_112-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-andy-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The MMR scare caused a low percentage of mumps vaccination (less than 30%), which keeps causing outbreaks in Japan.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naoko_111-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-naoko-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Up to 2002 measles caused deaths in Japan while there were none in UK, but it's apparently due to Japan applying the vaccine at a later age. A spokesman for the Ministry of Health said that the discontinuation had no effect in measles, although also saying that there were more deaths by measles while MMR was being used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whyjapan_110-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-whyjapan-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1994 the government dropped the vaccination requirement for measles and rubella due to the 1993 MMR scare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norrie_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-norrie-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :2
      </sup>
      Japan is nowadays the only developed country with large measles epidemics. It has been called a "measles exporter" by the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norrie_114-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-norrie-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As another consequence of the scare, in 2003, 7 million schoolchildren had not been vaccinated against rubella.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kakuchi_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kakuchi-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Autism rates continued to rise in Japan after the discontinuation of the MMR vaccine, which disproves any large-scale effect of vaccination,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andy_112-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-andy-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and means that the withdrawal of MMR in other countries is unlikely to cause a reduction in autism cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honda_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-honda-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Japanese government does not recognize any link between MMR and autism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whyjapan_110-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-whyjapan-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 2003 it was still trying to find a combined vaccine to replace MMR.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It was later discovered that some of the vaccines were administered after their expiry date and that the MMR compulsory vaccination was only retracted after the death of three children and more than 2000 reports of adverse effects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kakuchi_115-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-kakuchi-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1993 the Japanese government had paid $160,000 in compensation to the families of each of the three dead children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kakuchi_115-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-kakuchi-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other parents received no compensation because the government said that it was unproven that the MMR vaccine had been the cause; they decided to sue the manufacturer instead of the government.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kakuchi_115-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-kakuchi-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Osaka district court ruled on 13 March 2003 that the death of two children (among numerous other serious conditions) had been indeed caused by Japan's strain of Urabe MMR.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foiacentre.com_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-foiacentre.com-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2006, the Osaka High Court stated in another ruling that the state was responsible for failing to properly supervise a manufacturer of the measles-mumps-rubella vaccine, which caused severe side effects in children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="UK">
       UK
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: UK">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Commenced before the Civil Procedure Rules were promulgated, the MMR Litigation had its status as group litigation achieved by the then Lord Chief Justice's practice direction of 8 July 1999. On 8 June 2007, the High Court judge, Justice Keith, put an end to the group litigation because the withdrawal of legal aid by the legal services commission had made the pursuit of most of the claimants impossible. He ruled that all but two claims against pharmaceutical companies must be discontinued.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The judge stressed that his ruling did not amount to a rejection of any of the claims that MMR had seriously damaged the children concerned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_group" title="Pressure group">
       pressure group
      </a>
      called JABS (Justice, Awareness, Basic Support) was established to represent families with children who, their parents said, were "vaccine-damaged". 15million in public legal aid funding was spent on the litigation, of which 9.7million went to solicitors and
      <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">
       barristers
      </a>
      , and 4.3million to expert witnesses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick07_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick07-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Several British cases where parents claimed that their children had died as a result of Urabe MMR had received compensation under the "vaccine damage payment" scheme.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foiacentre.com_118-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-foiacentre.com-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
       United States
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: United States">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_court" title="Vaccine court">
       Vaccine court
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnibus_autism_proceeding" title="Omnibus autism proceeding">
       omnibus autism proceeding
      </a>
      (OAP)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      is a coordinated proceeding before the Office of Special Masters of the U.S. Court of Federal Claimscommonly called the
      <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_court" title="Vaccine court">
       vaccine court
      </a>
      . It is structured to facilitate the handling of nearly 5000 vaccine petitions involving claims that children who have received certain vaccinations have developed autism. The Petitioners' Steering Committee have claimed that MMR vaccines can cause autism, possibly in combination with
      <a href="/wiki/Thiomersal_controversy" title="Thiomersal controversy">
       thiomersal-containing vaccines
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2007 three test cases were presented to test the claims about the combination; these cases failed. The vaccine court ruled against the plaintiffs in all three cases, stating that the evidence presented did not validate their claims that vaccinations caused autism in these specific patients or in general.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cedillo-v-HHS_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cedillo-v-HHS-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In some cases, the plaintiffs' attorneys opted out of the Omnibus Autism Proceedings, which were concerned solely with autism, and issues concerned with bowel disorders; they argued their cases in the regular vaccine court.
     </p>
     <p>
      On 30 July 2007, the family of Bailey Banks, a child with pervasive developmental delay, won its case versus the Department of Health and Human Services.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a case listed as relating to 'non-autistic developmental delay', Special Master Richard B. Abell ruled that the Banks had successfully demonstrated that "the MMR vaccine at issue actually caused the conditions from which Bailey suffered and continues to suffer." In his conclusion, he ruled that he was satisfied that MMR had caused a brain inflammation called acute disseminated encephalomyelitis (ADEM). He reached this conclusion because of two vaccine cases in 1994 and 2001, which had concluded that "ADEM can be caused by natural measles, mumps, and rubella infections, as well as by measles, mumps, and rubella vaccines."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In other cases, attorneys did not claim that vaccines caused autism; they sought compensation for encephalopathy, encephalitis, or seizure disorders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Research">
       Research
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Research">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The number of reported cases of autism increased dramatically in the 1990s and early 2000s. This increase is largely attributable to changes in diagnostic practices; it is not known how much, if any, growth came from real changes in autism's
      <a href="/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">
       prevalence
      </a>
      , and no causal connection to the MMR vaccine has been demonstrated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rutter_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rutter-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2004, a meta review financed by the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
       European Union
      </a>
      assessed the evidence given in 120 other studies and considered unintended effects of the MMR vaccine, concluding that although the vaccine is associated with positive and negative side effects, a connection between MMR and autism was "unlikely".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2006 review of the literature regarding vaccines and autism found that "[t]he bulk of the evidence suggests no causal relationship between the MMR vaccine and autism."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2007 case study used the figure in Wakefield's 1999 letter to
      <i>
       The Lancet
      </i>
      alleging a temporal association between MMR vaccination and autism
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Original_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Original-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to illustrate how a graph can misrepresent its data, and gave advice to authors and publishers to avoid similar misrepresentations in the future.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2007 review of independent studies performed after the publication of Wakefield
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      's original report found that the studies provided compelling evidence against the hypothesis that MMR is associated with autism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A review of the work conducted in 2004 for UK court proceedings but not revealed until 2007 found that the
      <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">
       polymerase chain reaction
      </a>
      analysis essential to the Wakefield
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      results was fatally flawed due to contamination, and that it could not have possibly detected the measles that it was supposed to have detected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick07_123-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick07-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2009 review of studies on links between vaccines and autism discussed the MMR
      <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_controversies" title="Vaccine controversies">
       vaccine controversy
      </a>
      as one of three main hypotheses which epidemiological and biological studies failed to support.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2012, the
      <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_Library" title="Cochrane Library">
       Cochrane Library
      </a>
      published a review of dozens of scientific studies involving about 14,700,000 children, which found no credible evidence of an involvement of MMR with either autism or
      <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">
       Crohn's disease
      </a>
      . The authors stated that "the design and reporting of safety outcomes in MMR vaccine studies, both pre- and post-marketing, are largely inadequate".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane_12-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cochrane-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A June 2014
      <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">
       meta-analysis
      </a>
      involving more than 1.25 million children found that "vaccinations are not associated with the development of autism or autism spectrum disorder. Furthermore, the components of the vaccines (thimerosal or mercury) or multiple vaccines (MMR) are not associated with the development of autism or autism spectrum disorder."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2014_136-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2014-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In July 2014, a
      <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">
       systematic review
      </a>
      found "strong evidence that MMR vaccine is not associated with autism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       In April 2015 a study done in the United States of over 95,000 children assessing possible links between the MMR vaccine with autism concluded that there was no link.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)">
          <span title="Material near this tag may use an unreliable or less reliable medical source. (April 2015)">
           unreliable medical source?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         138
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         139
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_outbreaks">
       Disease outbreaks
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Disease outbreaks">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Measles_outbreaks_in_the_21st_century" title="Measles outbreaks in the 21st century">
       Measles outbreaks in the 21st century
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mumps_outbreaks_in_the_2000s" title="Mumps outbreaks in the 2000s">
       Mumps outbreaks in the 2000s
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      After the controversy began, the MMR vaccination compliance dropped sharply in the United Kingdom, from 92% in 1996 to 84% in 2002. In some parts of London, it was as low as 61% in 2003, far below the rate needed to avoid an epidemic of measles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 2006 coverage for MMR in the UK at 24 months was 85%, lower than the about 94% coverage for other vaccines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntyre_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After vaccination rates dropped, the incidence of two of the three diseases increased greatly in the UK. In 1998 there were 56 confirmed cases of measles in the UK; in 2006 there were 449 in the first five months of the year, with the first death since 1992; cases occurred in inadequately vaccinated children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mumps cases began rising in 1999 after years of very few cases, and by 2005 the United Kingdom was in a mumps epidemic with almost 5000 notifications in the first month of 2005 alone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The age group affected was too old to have received the routine MMR immunisations around the time the paper by Wakefield
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      was published, and too young to have contracted natural mumps as a child, and thus to achieve a
      <a href="/wiki/Herd_immunity" title="Herd immunity">
       herd immunity
      </a>
      effect. With the decline in mumps that followed the introduction of the MMR vaccine, these individuals had not been exposed to the disease, but still had no immunity, either natural or vaccine induced. Therefore, as immunisation rates declined following the controversy and the disease re-emerged, they were susceptible to infection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Measles and mumps cases continued in 2006, at incidence rates 13 and 37 times greater than respective 1998 levels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two children were severely and permanently injured by measles
      <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">
       encephalitis
      </a>
      despite undergoing
      <a href="/wiki/Kidney_transplantation" title="Kidney transplantation">
       kidney transplantation
      </a>
      in London.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pepys_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pepys-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Disease outbreaks also caused casualties in nearby countries. Three deaths and 1,500 cases were reported in the Irish outbreak of 2000, which occurred as a direct result of decreased vaccination rates following the MMR scare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pepys_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pepys-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008, for the first time in 14 years, measles was declared
      <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">
       endemic
      </a>
      in the UK, meaning that the disease was sustained within the population; this was caused by the preceding decade's low MMR vaccination rates, which created a population of susceptible children who could spread the disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eurosurveillance_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eurosurveillance-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      MMR vaccination rates for English children were unchanged in 200708 from the year before, at too low a level to prevent serious measles outbreaks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In May 2008, a British 17-year-old with an underlying
      <a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">
       immunodeficiency
      </a>
      died of measles. In 2008 Europe also faced a measles epidemic, including large outbreaks in Austria, Italy, and Switzerland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eurosurveillance_146-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eurosurveillance-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the January 2011
      <i>
       BMJ
      </i>
      statements of fraud by Wakefield,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Offit" title="Paul Offit">
       Paul Offit
      </a>
      , a pediatrician at
      <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Hospital_of_Philadelphia" title="Children's Hospital of Philadelphia">
       Children's Hospital of Philadelphia
      </a>
      and a "long-time critic of the dangers of the anti-vaccine movement", said, "that paper killed children",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_148-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Time-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KilledChildrenCBS_149-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-KilledChildrenCBS-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Michael Smith of the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Louisville" title="University of Louisville">
       University of Louisville
      </a>
      , an "infectious diseases expert who has studied the autism controversy's effect on immunization rates", said "clearly, the results of this (Wakefield) study have had repercussions."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APVaccineBooster_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-APVaccineBooster-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2014,
      <a href="/wiki/Laurie_Garrett" title="Laurie Garrett">
       Laurie Garrett
      </a>
      , senior fellow at the
      <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">
       Council on Foreign Relations
      </a>
      , blamed "Wakefieldism" for an increase in the number of unvaccinated children in countries such as Australia and New Zealand, saying, "Our data suggests that where Wakefield's message has caught on, measles follows."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_society">
       Impact on society
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=MMR_vaccine_controversy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Impact on society">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="New England Journal of Medicine">
        New England Journal of Medicine
       </a>
      </i>
      said that antivaccinationist activities resulted in a high cost to society, "including damage to individual and community well-being from outbreaks of previously controlled diseases, withdrawal of vaccine manufacturers from the market, compromising of national security (in the case of anthrax and smallpox vaccines), and lost productivity".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgeOld_23-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-AgeOld-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Costs to society from declining vaccination rates (in US dollars) were estimated by AOL's
      <i>
       Daily Finance
      </i>
      in 2011:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CostlyLie_154-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CostlyLie-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       A 20022003 outbreak of measles in Italy, "which led to the hospitalizations of more than 5,000 people, had a combined estimated cost between 17.6 million euros and 22.0 million euros".
      </li>
      <li>
       A 2004 outbreak of measles from "an unvaccinated student return[ing] from India in 2004 to Iowa was $142,452".
      </li>
      <li>
       A 2006 outbreak of mumps in Chicago, "caused by poorly immunized employees, cost the institution $262,788, or $29,199 per mumps case."
      </li>
      <li>
       A 2007 outbreak of mumps in Nova Scotia cost $3,511 per case.
      </li>
      <li>
       A 2008 outbreak of measles in San Diego, California cost $177,000, or $10,376 per case.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      In the United States,
      <a href="/wiki/Jenny_McCarthy" title="Jenny McCarthy">
       Jenny McCarthy
      </a>
      blamed vaccinations for her son Evan's disorders and leveraged her celebrity status to warn parents of a link between vaccines and autism. Evan's disorder began with seizures and his improvement occurred after the seizures were treated, symptoms experts have noted are more consistent with
      <a href="/wiki/Landau%E2%80%93Kleffner_syndrome" title="LandauKleffner syndrome">
       LandauKleffner syndrome
      </a>
      , often misdiagnosed as autism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenfeld_155-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenfeld-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the
      <i>
       Lancet
      </i>
      article was discredited, McCarthy continued to defend Wakefield.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParentsBelieve_156-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ParentsBelieve-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An article in Salon.com called McCarthy "a menace" for her continued position that vaccines are dangerous.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SalonMcCarthy_157-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SalonMcCarthy-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
